Mutawatir News: Definition and Necessary Knowledge
Definition of mutawatir and proof of its necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mutawatir news and the rational basis for its generation of necessary knowledge without reasoning.

Core Idea
Mutawatir news yields direct, undeniable knowledge without reasoning.

Sanusi now turns to a foundational category of transmitted knowledge: mutawatir news. He defines it precisely and shows why it produces certain knowledge without needing deduction. This passage establishes the epistemic bedrock for accepting reports about the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of mutawatir news. Note the condition that collusion on falsehood is rationally impossible.
The term 'tawāṭu’' (collusion) is key to the definition.
2
Examine the proof
Identify the proof that mutawatir gives knowledge: we find ourselves knowing about Mecca and Baghdad through reports alone.
This is an appeal to immediate experience.
3
Identify the two matters
Sanusi explicitly enumerates two points about mutawatir. Find them and see how they reinforce each other.
The first matter is about causation, the second about the nature of the knowledge.
4
Reflect on necessary knowledge
Consider why the knowledge is called necessary: it is obtained even by children who do not reason.
This distinguishes it from acquired (kasbī) knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو الخبر الثابت على السنة قوم لا يتصور تواطؤهم أى لا يجوز العقل توافقهم ( على الكذب ومصداقه : وقوع العلم من غير شبهة . وهو) بالضرورة ) موجب للعلم الضرورى، كالعلم بالملوك الخالية في الأزمنة الماضية . والبلدان النائية ) يحتمل العطف على الملوك، وعلى الأزمنة والأول أقرب . وإن كان أبعد فههنا أمران. أحدهما : أن المتواتر موجب للعلم وذلك بالضرورة فإنا نجد من أنفسنا العلم بوجود (مكة) و (بغداد) وأنه ليس إلا بالإخبار (۱). والثاني: أن العلم الحاصل به ضرورى . وذلك لأنه يحصل للمستدل وغيره، حتى الصبيان الذين لا اهتداء لهم بطريق الاكتساب وترتيب المقدمات (۲).
And it is the news established on the tongues of a people whose collusion cannot be imagined, i.e., it is not rationally permissible for them to agree on falsehood. Its verification is the occurrence of knowledge without doubt. (And it) necessarily causes necessary knowledge, like knowledge of past kings in bygone times and distant countries. (It is possible to read it as either referring to kings or times, the former being closer.) Though the other is more distant, there are two matters here. One: that mutawatir causes knowledge, and that by necessity, for we find in ourselves knowledge of the existence of Mecca and Baghdad, and that it is only through reports (1). Two: that the knowledge obtained through it is necessary. This is because it is obtained by the reasoner and others, even children who have no guidance to the method of acquisition and arrangement of premises (2).
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines mutawatir news and explains its epistemic status. It first defines mutawatir as news transmitted by a group whose collusion on falsehood is rationally impossible, and its result is certain knowledge (al-‘ilm bi lā shubhah). It then states that mutawatir produces necessary (darūrī) knowledge, giving examples: knowledge of past kings and distant cities like Mecca and Baghdad. This knowledge arises by necessity from the reports themselves, not through reasoning. The passage further clarifies two points: first, that mutawatir causes knowledge necessarily, as seen in the immediate knowledge of the existence of these cities; second, that the resulting knowledge is necessary because it is obtained even by those who do not engage in reasoning, such as children. Thus, it is not a product of deduction but a direct cognition.

Key Terms
الخبر المتواتر (al-khabar al-mutawātir, recurrent news)
تواطؤ (tawāṭu', collusion)
العلم الضروري (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī, necessary knowledge)
المستدل (al-mustadill, reasoner)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All reports yield necessary knowledge.
Correction: Only mutawatir reports produce necessary knowledge; other reports may only yield speculative knowledge.

Checkpoint
What type of knowledge does mutawatir news produce?
A) Speculative knowledge
B) Necessary knowledge
C) Deductive knowledge
D) Imitation

Key Takeaway
Mutawatir news is the only type of report that yields necessary knowledge without reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you as certain of the truth of Islam as you are of the existence of Mecca? If not, what is the gap, and how does this lesson challenge you to close it?
?
Think of a truth you accepted as a child without proof—does your faith in Allah hold that same immediate certainty? If not, what would it take for you to experience that certainty now?
?
If mutawatir news gives necessary knowledge, how does that affect your confidence in the transmission of the Quran and Sunnah? Have you ever doubted any essential aspect of the religion despite clear, mass-transmitted reports? What would change if you truly internalized this lesson?

Objection to Mutawatir Based on Probability and Its Answer
Objection and answer on mutawatir probability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that probability cannot yield certainty is answered using the rope analogy.

Core Idea
Adding probabilities can yield certainty when they cohere, like strands of a rope.

This passage presents a key objection against the reliability of mutawatir reports: that individual reports only give probability, and stacking probabilities cannot produce certainty. The answer uses a powerful analogy to show that combination creates something new. Let's follow the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the specific examples of Christians and Jews.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The passage is clearly divided into an objection (starting with 'فإن قيل') and an answer ('قلنا'). Pinpoint where each begins.
3
Analyze the two sub-arguments of the objection
The objection has two parts: (1) probability piling doesn't give certainty, (2) possibility of lying in each implies possibility in the whole. Understand each.
The first is about certainty, the second about collective falsehood.
4
Examine the analogical answer
The answer uses the analogy of a rope made of weak hairs. Compare the structure: many weak strands combine to produce strength. Relate this to reports: many probable reports combine to produce certainty.
This is the key: combination produces a new quality.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: what does this analogy imply about the nature of tawatur? Why is it a valid refutation?
The answer does not deny the premises; it shows a flaw in the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما خبر النصاري بقتل عيسى - عليه السلام - واليهود بتابید دین موسى - عليه السلام - فتواتره ممنوع فإن قيل : خبر كل واحد لا يفيد إلا الظن. وضم الظن إلى الظن لا يوجب اليقين. وأيضا : جواز كذب كل واحد يوجب جواز كذب المجموع لأنه نفس الآحاد . قلنا : ربما يكون مع الاجتماع ما لا يكون مع الانفراد، كقوة الحبل المؤلف من الشعرات (۱).
As for the report of the Christians that ‘Īsā (peace be upon him) was killed, and the Jews that the religion of Mūsā (peace be upon him) is permanent, their tawātur is denied. For it is said: The report of each individual does not give more than probability. And adding probability to probability does not produce certainty. Also: The possibility of lying in each individual implies the possibility of lying in the whole, because it is the same individuals. We say: Sometimes with combination there occurs what does not occur with singularity, like the strength of a rope made of hairs (1).
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding mutawatir. The objection first denies the tawātur of the Christians' report about Jesus's crucifixion and the Jews' report about the perpetuity of Moses's religion. Then it argues that a single report gives only probability, and piling probabilities cannot yield certainty. Moreover, since each reporter could lie, the whole group could also lie. The answer responds by analogy: just as a rope made of many thin hairs gains strength from combination, so multiple reports can produce certainty even if each is merely probable. The collective recurrence transcends the individual limitations.

Key Terms
الظن (al-ẓann, probability)
اليقين (al-yaqīn, certainty)
جواز الكذب (jawāz al-kadhib, possibility of lying)
اجتماع (ijtimā‘, combination)
الانفراد (al-infirād, singularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that adding probabilities can never produce certainty, treating collective reports as simply the sum of individual probabilities.
Correction: The rope analogy shows that combination can create a new quality (strength) not present in individual strands; similarly, tawātur yields certainty from probable reports.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that adding probabilities cannot yield certainty?
A) By denying the premise that individual reports are probable
B) By using the analogy of a rope made of hairs
C) By citing revelation that confirms the reports
D) By saying all reports are certain from the start

Key Takeaway
The rope analogy demonstrates that combination can produce a qualitatively new result, countering the probability objection against tawātur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Islam that came from a 'rope' of many testimonies. How does the rope analogy affect your confidence in that belief? Are you willing to act on it as if it were certain?
?
When you hear a report from many sources, do you instinctively treat it as more reliable, or do you still doubt? The objection says 'adding probabilities doesn't give certainty'. Which side of the argument reflects your actual behavior?
?
The passage says 'combination can produce what does not occur with singularity'. Is there an area in your spiritual life where you have seen a collective practice (like congregation prayer, group study, or community testimony) produce a certainty that you could not achieve alone? How can you seek that more intentionally?

Objection to Uniformity of Necessary Knowledge and Its Answer
Objection that necessary knowledge is uniform and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that necessary knowledge can vary in strength due to factors like habit and practice, and that denial of mutawatir knowledge is due to obstinacy.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge is not uniform; it varies by human factors like habit and attention.

This passage addresses an objection that necessary knowledge must be uniform and admits no gradation. The objector points to varying certainty in different truths and to sects that reject mutawatir. The answer refutes the premise and explains that variation arises from familiarity, practice, and mental focus, while outright denial is mere obstinacy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that necessary knowledge should be uniform without gradation, yet our experience shows variation. For example, knowledge that one is half of two is stronger than knowledge of Alexander's existence. Additionally, groups like the Sumaniyya and Brahmins deny that mutawatir gives knowledge.
This challenges the reliability of mutawatir reports.
2
Analyze the answer
The author denies the premise that necessary knowledge is uniform. He explains that types of necessary knowledge can vary due to differences in familiarity, habit, practice, bringing to mind, and mental representation of the boundaries of judgments.
3
Consider the rebuttal of denial
The author attributes the denial of mutawatir by some rational people to obstinacy and stubbornness, akin to the Sophists who reject all necessary truths. Thus, such denial does not undermine the necessity of mutawatir knowledge.
This shows that the objector's evidence is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الضروريات لا يقع فيها التفاوت ولا الاختلاف، ونحن نجد العلم يكون الواحد نصف الاثنين، أقوى من العلم بوجود «اسكندر» والمتواتر قد أنكر إفادته العلم جماعة من العقلاء. كالسمنية والبراهمة قلنا ممنوع بل قد تتفاوت أنواع الضروري، بواسطة التفاوت في الإلف والعادة ، والممارسة، والإخطار بالبال وتصورات أطراف الأحكام. وقد يختلف فيه مكابرة وعنادا، كالسوفسطائية في جميع الضروريات .
For it is said: Necessary matters do not admit of gradation or difference, yet we find that the knowledge that one is half of two is stronger than the knowledge of the existence of Alexander, and mutawatir has been denied to give knowledge by some rational people, like the Sumaniyya and the Brahmins. We say: It is denied; rather, types of the necessary may vary due to variation in familiarity, habit, practice, bringing to mind, and mental representation of the boundaries of judgments. And one may differ about it obstinately and stubbornly, like the Sophists in all necessary matters.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: necessary knowledge should be uniform and not admit degrees, but we observe that knowledge of basic arithmetic is stronger than knowledge of historical facts like Alexander's existence. Also, some rational sects (Sumaniyya and Brahmins) deny that mutawatir gives knowledge. The answer denies the premise that necessary knowledge is uniform. Rather, necessary knowledge can vary in strength due to factors like habituation (ulf), practice (mumārasah), attention (al-ikhtār bi al-bāl), and mental grasp of the implications. The denial by some is attributed to obstinacy and stubbornness, akin to the Sophists who reject all self-evident truths. Thus, the variation does not undermine the necessity of knowledge from mutawatir.

Key Terms
الضروريات (al-ḍarūriyyāt, necessary truths)
التفاوت (al-tafāwut, gradation)
الإلف والعادة (al-ulf wa al-‘ādah, familiarity and habit)
الممارسة (al-mumārasah, practice)
السمنية (al-Sumaniyya)
البراهمة (al-Barāhima, Brahmins)
السوفسطائية (al-Sūfisṭā’iyyah, Sophists)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge must be uniform and cannot vary in strength.
Correction: Necessary knowledge can vary in strength due to factors like habit, practice, and attention; denial occurs from obstinacy.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why might some deny that mutawatir gives knowledge?
A) Because they lack evidence
B) Because of obstinacy like the Sophists
C) Because mutawatir is not necessary
D) Because knowledge is always the same

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge admits gradation due to human factors, and denial of mutawatir is rooted in obstinacy, not deficiency in the evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there Islamic truths you hold strongly simply because you have heard them repeatedly, while other equally authentic teachings have little impact on your heart? What does this passage reveal about the role of habit in your certainty? How can you intentionally seek to strengthen your conviction in teachings you tend to neglect?
?
Have you ever dismissed a true report from the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wasallam) because it seemed less certain to you than other matters, perhaps due to lack of familiarity or personal resistance? This passage shows that variation in certainty does not invalidate the evidence. What would it take for you to submit fully to such reports, even when they challenge your habits or preferences?

The Scope of Reports That Yield Knowledge
The author responds to an objection about the types of reports that yield knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to an objection about the types of reports that yield knowledge, and how reports from God, angels, or consensus relate to the two main categories.

Core Idea
Not every certain report is a source of necessary knowledge by itself.

The discussion on knowledge-producing reports continues with an objection: why limit knowledge-yielding reports to just two types? The objector lists several other candidates. The author clarifies what is meant by 'report' in this context, showing that reports from God, angels, or consensus ultimately fall under the same categories or require additional proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that true reports yielding knowledge are not only the two types already discussed; they could include reports from God, angels, consensus, or reports with contextual clues.
Notice the examples given: report of God, angel, ahl al-ijma', and a report about Zayd's arrival.
2
Understand the author's main answer
The author replies that 'report' here means a report that yields knowledge for all people simply by being a report, without considering external clues or proofs.
This definition excludes reports that rely on circumstantial evidence.
3
Examine the handling of divine and angelic reports
Reports from God or angels only benefit people when they reach them through the Prophet, so they take the ruling of prophetic reports.
Thus they are not an independent category.
4
See how consensus reports are treated
The report of ahl al-ijma' is considered like mutawatir in ruling, though some argue it only yields knowledge after considering proofs that consensus is a proof.
The author points out that even prophetic reports are inferential in this sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الخبر الصادق المفيد للعلم لا ينحصر في النوعين، بل قد يكون خبر الله تعالى، أو خبر الملك أو خبر أهل الإجماع، أو الخبر المقرون بما يرفع احتمال الكذب. كالخبر بقدوم «زيد ) عند تسارع قومه إلى داره . قلنا : المراد بالخبر خبر يكون سبب العلم لعامة الخلق بمجرد كونه خبرا مع قطع النظر عن القرائن المفيدة لليقين بدلالة العقل. فخبر الله تعالى أو خبر الملك، إنما يكون مفيدا للعلم بالنسبة إلى عامة الخلق، إذا وصل إليهم من جهة الرسول عليه السلام، فحكمه خبر الرسول . وخبر أهل الإجماع في حكم المتواتر. وقد يجاب بأنه لا يفيد ،بمجرده بل فى النظر في الأدلة الدالة على كون الإجماع حجة . قلنا : وكذلك خبر الرسول، لهذا جعل استدلاليا .
If it is said: The true report that yields knowledge is not confined to the two types; rather it may be the report of God Almighty, or the report of an angel, or the report of the people of consensus (ahl al-ijma'), or a report accompanied by what removes the possibility of lying, like the report of the arrival of Zayd when his people rush to his house. We say: What is meant by 'report' is a report that is a cause of knowledge for all people simply by being a report, apart from the contextual clues that yield certainty by indication of reason. So the report of God Almighty or the report of an angel only yields knowledge for all people when it reaches them through the Messenger, peace be upon him; thus its ruling is that of the report of the Messenger. And the report of the people of consensus is like the mutawatir in ruling. And it may be answered that it does not yield knowledge by itself, but by considering the proofs that indicate that consensus is a proof. We say: Likewise the report of the Messenger, for this reason it is considered inferential.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and answer regarding the types of reports that produce knowledge. The objector claims that a true report giving knowledge is not limited to two types (mutawatir and directly heard); it can be a report from God, an angel, the people of consensus (ijma'), or a report accompanied by circumstantial evidence that precludes lying, such as seeing people rushing to Zayd's house when his arrival is reported. The author replies that the intended meaning of 'report' in this context is one that yields knowledge for all people simply by being a report, without considering external clues that give certainty through reason. Reports from God or angels only benefit people when they reach them through the Prophet, so they fall under the ruling of prophetic reports. The report of ahl al-ijma' is considered like mutawatir. Alternatively, it can be said that such a report does not yield knowledge by itself but only after considering the proofs that ijma' is a proof. The author concludes: the same applies to the Prophet's report, which is why it is considered inferential (istidlali) for its content.

Key Terms
الخبر الصادق
خبر الله
خبر الملك
أهل الإجماع
القرائن
المتواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any report accompanied by certainty-producing clues yields necessary knowledge immediately, without considering the report itself.
Correction: The author clarifies that the report itself must be the sole cause of knowledge for it to be considered a knowledge-yielding report; clues that produce certainty are external and thus the report is not inherently certain.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does the report of God or an angel yield knowledge for all people?
A) Immediately, because it is from God or an angel
B) Only when it reaches them through the Messenger
C) Only when accompanied by contextual clues
D) It never yields knowledge for all people

Key Takeaway
Reports from God, angels, or consensus are not independent categories; they ultimately depend on the Prophet or additional proofs to yield knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a verse of the Quran, do you feel an immediate certainty, or do you recognize that it reached you through the Prophet and requires reasoning about his truthfulness? How does that affect your conviction?
?
The author says a report only yields knowledge 'simply by being a report' if it gives knowledge to all people. Does your faith depend on such a report, or on proofs and signs? Have you ever tested the foundations of your belief rationally?
?
If the Prophet's report is inferential, then your certainty about his message rests on a proof. Are you actively seeking to strengthen that proof, or do you rely on inherited confidence? What would it take for you to be able to defend the truth of his report with reasoning?

Meaning of Hadith and the Philosophers' Interpretation
Defines hadith as preceded by non-existence and contrasts philosophers' view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'muhdath' in Sunni theology and how it differs from the philosophers' usage of 'huduth'.

Core Idea
Philosophers and theologians use the same word 'huduth' with different meanings.

This passage introduces the foundational term 'muhdath' (temporally originated) as defined by Sunni theologians. It then contrasts this with the philosophers' claim that the heavens and elements are pre-eternal, and explains that when philosophers say everything other than Allah is 'hadith', they mean something else entirely. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid confusion in later theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of muhdath
The passage begins by defining 'muhdath' as that which is brought from non-existence to existence. Note the phrase 'مخرج من العدم إلى الوجود'.
This is the standard Ash'ari definition.
2
Examine the philosophers' view
The philosophers hold that the heavens and elements are pre-eternal (qadim) in their matter, form, and shape as species. They never lack a form at any time.
This contradicts the Sunni view that everything other than Allah is created in time.
3
Grasp the equivocation on huduth
Notice that philosophers still use the term 'huduth' for everything other than Allah, but they mean 'need for another' (ihtiyaj ila al-ghayr), not 'being preceded by non-existence'. This is a key difference.
Be careful not to assume agreement when the same term is used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(محدث) أى مخرج من العدم إلى الوجود. بمعنى أنه كان معدوما، فوجد، خلافا للفلاسفة. حيث ذهبوا إلى قدم السماوات بموادها وصورها وأشكالها، وقدم العناصر بموادها وصورها، لكن بالنوع بمعنى أنها لم تخل عن صورة قط عن صورة ما .
نعم أطلقوا القول بحدوث ما سوى الله تعالى، لكن بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الغير، لا بمعنى سبق العدم عليه.
(Muhdath) meaning brought out from non-existence into existence. Meaning that it was non-existent and then came into existence, contrary to the philosophers. For they held the pre-eternity of the heavens with their matters and forms and shapes, and the pre-eternity of the elements with their matters and forms, but in terms of species, meaning that they never lacked a form for any form. Yes, they applied the term 'huduth' to everything other than Allah, but in the sense of need for another, not in the sense of being preceded by non-existence.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'muhdath' (temporally originated) as that which is brought from non-existence into existence, i.e., it was non-existent and then came to be. This is the standard Ash'ari definition. The commentator then contrasts this with the philosophers' position. The philosophers held that the heavens and the elements are pre-eternal (qadim) with respect to their matter and form as species—they never lack a form at any time. However, they do apply the term 'huduth' to everything other than Allah, but in a different sense: they mean by it 'need for another' (ihtiyaj ila al-ghayr), not 'being preceded by non-existence'. This distinction is crucial because it shows that the philosophers do not affirm that the world is created in time ex nihilo; they merely acknowledge ontological dependency. The passage thus guards against equivocation on the term 'hadith'.

Key Terms
محدث
حدوث
قدم
احتياج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That philosophers and theologians agree on the temporality of the world because they both use the term 'hadith'.
Correction: The passage explains that the philosophers mean something different by 'huduth'—they mean need for another (ihtiyaj) rather than being preceded by non-existence, so their position is not the same as the theologians'.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what do the philosophers mean by 'huduth'?
A) Being preceded by non-existence
B) Need for another (ihtiyaj ila al-ghayr)
C) Being created ex nihilo
D) Having a beginning in time

Key Takeaway
The Sunni definition of 'muhdath' is 'preceded by non-existence', whereas the philosophers use 'huduth' to mean 'need for another'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You use the word 'created' to describe the universe. But do you truly mean 'brought from nothing by Allah's will', or have you unconsciously adopted a view that the universe is merely 'dependent' on Allah without a clear beginning? Search your heart: do you live as if everything around you—your own existence—began from absolute nothingness by Allah's command?
?
The philosophers and theologians used the same word 'huduth' yet meant fundamentally different things. Are there any terms in your own faith—like 'qadr' or 'iman'—that you use loosely, assuming others mean the same, while in reality you might be undermining a core teaching? Will you now examine your definitions with the same precision this lesson demands?
?
If the heavens and the earth were truly 'preceded by non-existence' and brought into being by Allah, then your very breath at this moment is a fresh act of creation. Does your daily life reflect this moment-by-moment need for Allah, or do you fall into the illusion of self-sufficiency? What will you change today to live with constant awareness that you are, at every instant, being created from nothing?

The Threefold Scriptural Proof for Seeing Allah
Scriptural evidence that believers will see God in the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of transmitted evidence (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma') that establish the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Scripture provides threefold proof that believers will see their Lord.

The vision of Allah in the afterlife is a doctrine affirmed by transmitted evidence. In this passage, the author cites the Quran, a famous hadith, and the consensus of the early community. This lesson walks through each proof and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three types of evidence mentioned.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage explicitly names three sources: the Book (Quran), the Sunnah (hadith), and consensus (ijma'). Locate each one.
3
Examine the Quranic verse
The verse from Surah al-Qiyamah describes faces looking at their Lord. Consider how this literally implies vision.
4
Examine the hadith
The hadith compares seeing God to seeing the full moon. Note the large number of narrators (21 Companions).
5
Understand the role of consensus
The author states that the early community unanimously agreed on the occurrence of vision. Consensus is a third independent proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجبة بالنقل . ورد الدليل السمعى بايجاب رؤية المؤمنين الله في دار الآخرة أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى: ﴿ وجوه يومئذ ناضرة * إلى ربها ناظرة ﴾ [القيامة : ٢٢، ٢٣] وأما السنة فقوله عليه السلام إنكم سترون ربكم كما ترون القمر ليلة البدر ) وهو مشهور. رواه واحد وعشرون من أكابر الصحابة رضى الله عنهم. وأما الإجماع فهو أن الأمة كانوا مجمعين على وقوع الرؤية في
It is obligatory by transmitted evidence. The scriptural proof came establishing the vision of believers of God in the abode of the afterlife. As for the Book, it is His statement: 'Faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (Quran 75:22-23). As for the Sunnah, it is his statement, peace be upon him: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon'—and it is famous. Twenty-one of the senior Companions, may God be pleased with them, narrated it. As for consensus, it is that the community was unanimously agreed upon the occurrence of vision in

Commentary
This passage provides the transmitted proof for the vision of God in the afterlife. The author states that the vision is obligatory (wajib) by transmitted evidence (naql). He then presents three types of evidence: 
1. The Quran: verse 75:22-23, which describes faces looking at their Lord. 
2. The Sunnah: The famous hadith that believers will see God as clearly as seeing the full moon, narrated by twenty-one senior Companions. 
3. Consensus (ijma'): The Muslim community was unanimously agreed that vision will occur in the afterlife. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing to explain the consensus.

Key Terms
الرؤية (vision)
واجبة (obligatory)
النقل (transmitted evidence)
الإجماع (consensus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse and hadith should be interpreted metaphorically, not as literal sight.
Correction: Classical Sunni theology takes them as literal proof for actual vision, based on the apparent meaning and consensus.

A) Twelve
B) Twenty-one
C) Thirty
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The vision of Allah in the afterlife is firmly established by three transmitted proofs: Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The promise of seeing Allah is certain from Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. If you truly believed that one day you will look at your Lord with your own eyes, would your life look different right now? What is one specific habit you will change today because of this certainty?
?
Knowing that the entire early community agreed on this vision, do you find yourself ever doubting or rationalizing it away? Where are you trusting your own reasoning over their consensus? What will you do this week to strengthen your conviction in this unseen reality?
?
Imagine the moment your face becomes radiant as you turn to see your Lord. Are you living in a way that seeks that radiance? What is one hidden sin or distraction you must remove starting today so that you are not ashamed on that Day?

The Objection of Musa's Request and Its Refutation
Author refutes the objection that the story of Musa negates the possibility of ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of ru'ya by answering the strongest objection based on the story of Moses.

Core Idea
Conditional possibility does not imply impossibility.

This passage addresses the most powerful objection against the possibility of seeing Allah—the story of Moses asking to see Him. The author carefully unpacks the objection and shows why it fails to prove impossibility. By analyzing his response, you will see how careful reasoning about conditional statements and context resolves apparent scriptural conflicts.

Guided Path
1
State the core claim
The vision (ru'ya) is conditioned upon the mountain's stability. The author asserts this is possible because the condition itself is possible.
This is the foundation of the argument.
2
Present the objection
The strongest objection is that Moses asked for ru'ya to teach his people, implying impossibility, and that the condition is impossible (mountain stability while moving).
Two parts: purpose of request and impossibility of condition.
3
Examine the answer to the first part
The author argues that whether the people were believers or disbelievers, Moses asking was futile—thus the objection does not prove impossibility.
This refutes the idea that the request was to teach impossibility.
4
Examine the answer to the second part
Stability while moving is not inherently impossible; stillness could replace motion. Only simultaneous motion and stillness is impossible.
This shows the condition could be possible under another state.
5
Conclude
The objections fail to negate the possibility of ru'ya, so the original argument stands.
The author's reasoning is consistent with scripture and logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرؤية باستقرار الجبل (۱) ، وهو أمر ممكن فى نفسه. والمعلق بالممكن ممكن لأن معناه الإخبار بثبوت المعلق عند ثبوت المعلق به، والمجال لا يثبت على شيء من التقادير الممكنة. وقد اعترض عليه بوجوه أقواها : أن سؤال موسى عليه السلام كان لاجل قومه، حيث :قالوا : لن نؤمن لك حتى نرى الله جهرة ﴾ [البقرة: ٥٥] فسأل ليعلموا ﴿لَن تُؤْمِنَ امتناعها، كما علمه هو . وبأنا لا نسلم أن المعلق عليه ممكن، بل هو استقرار الجبل حال تحركه وهو محال وأجيب بأن كلا من ذلك خلاف الظاهر، ولا ضرورة في ارتكابه ، على أن القوم إن كانوا مؤمنين كفاهم قول موسى عليه السلام أن الرؤية ممتنعة . وإن كانوا كفارا، لم يصدقوه في حكم الله تعالى بالامتناع، وأيا ما كان يكون السؤال عبثا والاستقرار حال التحرك أيضا ممكن بأن يقع السكون بدل الحركة ، وإنما المحال اجتماع الحركة والسكون
The vision (ru'ya) is conditional upon the mountain's stability (istiqrar), and that is a matter possible in itself. The dependent (al-mu'allaq) upon the possible is possible, because its meaning is informing of the establishment of the dependent when the thing upon which it depends (al-mu'allaq 'alayh) is established, and the impossible does not occur under any possible estimation. And an objection was raised against it with several aspects, the strongest of which: that the question of Moses (peace be upon him) was for the sake of his people, when they said, 'We will not believe you until we see Allah plainly' (Quran 2:55). So he asked so that they would know its impossibility, just as he knew it. And [secondly] that we do not concede that the thing upon which it depends is possible; rather it is the stability of the mountain while it is moving, and that is impossible. And it was answered that each of these is contrary to the apparent meaning (zahir), and there is no necessity in adopting it, given that if the people were believers, the word of Moses that the vision is impossible would suffice them; and if they were disbelievers, they would not have believed him in Allah's judgment of impossibility, and in either case the question would be futile. And [further] the stability while moving is also possible, by the occurrence of stillness instead of motion; only the simultaneous gathering of motion and stillness is impossible.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument defending the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. The author first states that the vision of Allah is made conditional upon the mountain's stability in the Quranic verse (Quran 7:143). He argues that this conditional is possible because the condition (stability of the mountain) is not inherently impossible. The dependent (the vision) being conditional upon a possible thing makes it also possible, because the meaning of a conditional statement is simply that the dependent occurs when the condition occurs, and impossibility does not arise from such a conditional. Then he presents the strongest objection: Moses' request was actually for his people who demanded to see Allah plainly (Quran 2:55); Moses already knew it was impossible, so he asked to teach them its impossibility. Additionally, the objector denies that the condition (the mountain's stability) is possible at the moment of its being made to appear? Actually the objection says: we do not concede that the condition (al-mu'allaq 'alayh) is possible; rather, it is the mountain's stability while it is moving, and that is impossible (because the mountain was made to quake). The author answers that both of these objections depart from the apparent meaning of the text, and there is no necessity to adopt such departures. Regarding the first, if the people were believers, Moses' word would suffice; if disbelievers, they wouldn't believe him, so the request would be futile in either case. Regarding the second, stability while moving is not necessarily impossible; it could be that stillness replaces motion, and only the simultaneous existence of motion and stillness is impossible. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الرؤية (ru'ya)
المعلق (al-mu'allaq)
المعلق عليه (al-mu'allaq 'alayh)
الاستقرار (al-istiqrar)
سؤال موسى (su'al Musa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic conditional (if the mountain remains stable, you will see Me) means that ru'ya is absolutely impossible because the condition cannot be fulfilled.
Correction: The author argues that the condition is possible in itself, and the conditional does not necessitate impossibility; it only makes ru'ya dependent on a possible event.

Checkpoint
What is the author's answer to the objection that Moses asked to see Allah to teach his people the impossibility of ru'ya?
A) Moses himself was unaware of the impossibility.
B) In either case (people were believers or disbelievers), the question would be futile.
C) The people already knew the vision was possible.
D) The Quranic verse was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The strongest objection to ru'ya fails because the conditional on a possible event does not prove impossibility, and the context of Moses' request does not negate the possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the strongest objection to seeing Allah was refuted. But have you ever, in your heart, doubted that you could see Him? What is the state of your certainty about the afterlife? Sit with this passage until you feel the possibility of ru'ya settle in your soul. Then let that hope transform your worship today.
?
Moses' people demanded to see Allah out of stubbornness. When you pray, do you ever feel a similar demand for immediate proof? This lesson shows that the door to ru'ya remains open by Allah's power. Will you ask Allah tonight to grant you the vision of Him in Paradise, and to purify your heart from the need for proof in this world?
?
The author argues that conditional possibility does not imply impossibility. Consider the promises Allah has made to you: are you living as if they are impossible, or as if they are possible through His power? Pick one promise you struggle to believe and meditate on why it is possible, then change one action today to reflect that belief.

Scriptural Proofs for the Vision of Allah in the Afterlife
Author cites Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma' to prove ru'ya is wajib in the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scriptural proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma') that establish the vision of Allah in the Hereafter as an obligatory belief.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah in the afterlife is proven by Quran, Sunnah, and early consensus.

In this passage, the author presents the transmitted evidence (naql) for the belief that believers will see Allah in the afterlife. He cites a clear Quranic verse, a famous prophetic hadith, and the consensus of the early Muslim community. This lesson unpacks each proof and explains why they are decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author structures his argument around three sources: Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma'.
The author states the claim first: it is wajib by naql.
2
Examine the Quranic verse and its interpretation
Focus on the verse from Surah al-Qiyamah (75:22-23). The word 'nazira' is interpreted as actual sight. Understand why this verse is a clear proof.
The verse says faces will be 'looking at their Lord'.
3
Understand the role of hadith and consensus
The hadith about seeing the Lord like the full moon is narrated by many Companions, indicating its authenticity. The consensus of the early community confirms the literal understanding.
The author notes that later opponents appeared, but the early consensus was on the literal meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجبة (٢) بالنقل. ورد الدليل السمعى بايجاب رؤية المؤمنين الله في دار الآخرة أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى: ﴿ وجوه يومئذ ناضرة * إلى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ﴾ [القيامة: ٢٢، ٢٣] وأما السنة فقوله عليه السلام إنكم سترون ربكم كما ترون القمر ليلة البدر ) وهو مشهور. رواه واحد وعشرون من أكابر الصحابة رضى الله عنهم. وأما الإجماع فهو أن الأمة كانوا مجمعين على وقوع الرؤية في الآخرة (١) ، وأن الآيات الواردة في ذلك محمولة على ظواهرها، ثم ظهرت مقالة المخالفين وشاعت شبههم وتأويلاتهم.
It is obligatory (wajiba) by transmission (naql). The scriptural proof (al-dalil al-sam'i) has come affirming the vision (ru'ya) of the believers of Allah in the abode of the Hereafter. As for the Book, it is His statement, the Most High: 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (Quran 75:22-23). And as for the Sunnah, it is his statement (peace be upon him): 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon'—and it is well-known. Twenty-one of the leading Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) narrated it. And as for the consensus (ijma'), it is that the community (umma) were in agreement upon the occurrence of the vision in the Hereafter, and that the verses that have come concerning that are to be taken upon their apparent meanings (zawahir), then the doctrine of the opponents appeared and their doubts and interpretations became widespread.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural proofs (al-dalil al-sam'i) that establish the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the Hereafter as obligatory belief. The author first states the claim: it is wajib by naql (transmitted evidence). Then he provides three sources: (1) The Quran: verse 75:22-23, which says that faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord. The word 'nazira' is interpreted as looking with the eyes. (2) The Sunnah: the famous hadith that the believers will see their Lord like they see the full moon, narrated by twenty-one Companions. (3) Consensus (ijma'): the early Muslim community agreed upon the occurrence of the vision in the Hereafter and that the relevant verses are to be understood literally (zawahir). The author notes that afterwards, the opponents appeared and their doubts and allegorical interpretations became widespread. This shows that the early consensus was on the literal meaning, and later innovations are rejected.

Key Terms
واجبة (wajiba)
النقل (naql)
الدليل السمعي (al-dalil al-sam'i)
الرؤية (ru'ya)
الإجماع (ijma')
الظواهر (zawahir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that the consensus on ru'ya was not early, but rather emerged later.
Correction: The author asserts that the umma was in agreement before the appearance of opposing views, indicating that the consensus was early and authoritative.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many Companions narrated the hadith that believers will see their Lord like the full moon?
A) Ten
B) Twenty-one
C) Thirty
D) The number is not mentioned

Key Takeaway
The belief that believers will see Allah in the afterlife is firmly established by clear scriptural evidence and early consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly yearn for the vision of Allah in the afterlife, or has the busyness of this world dulled your longing? What must change in your heart today to make that vision your greatest desire?
?
The Companions unanimously believed in this vision. Are you willing to submit to their consensus even when your mind or the doubters around you question it? Take a concrete step this week to strengthen your certainty in this unseen reality.
?
When you read the verse 'Faces on that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord,' can you honestly say your soul longs for that moment? If not, what attachment in your life is blocking that hope, and what will you do about it today?

The Conditions of Vision and Rational Objections
Author denies conditions for Allah's vision and refutes rational objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conditions required for physical vision do not apply to Allah, and how the rational objection based on necessity is answered in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Allah is seen without place, direction, or distance; vision is by His creation, not by conditions.

Sanusi has affirmed that believers will see Allah in the Hereafter. Now he addresses the objections that arise from applying the conditions of physical sight to Allah. The first set of objections is rational: they assume that vision requires certain conditions like place, direction, and distance. The author denies these conditions and refutes the analogy used to support them. Then he answers the objection that if vision were possible, it would be necessary when conditions are met.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the list of denied conditions and the rational objection that follows.
2
Identify the denied conditions
Identify the five conditions that are denied for vision of Allah: place, direction, facing, connection of a ray, and distance.
3
Understand the refutation of analogy
The author states that analogizing the unseen to the witnessed is invalid. Understand why this is key.
4
Analyze the rational objection and answer
The objection says vision would be necessary if possible. The answer says vision is by Allah's creation, so it is not necessitated by conditions.
This is a crucial point: vision is a direct act of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيرى لا فى مكان ولا في جهة من مقابلة أو اتصال شعاع ولا بثبوت مسافة بين الرائى وبين الله تعالى وقياس الغائب على الشاهد فاسد (٢). وقد يستدل على عدم الاشتراط برؤية الله تعالى إيانا وفيه نظر لأن الكلام فى الرؤية بحاسة البصر. فإن قيل لو كان جائز الرؤية والحاسة سليمة وسائر الشرائط موجودة لوجب أن يرى وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شاهقة لا نراها وأنها سفسطة .. قلنا ممنوع فإن الرؤية عندنا بخلق الله تعالى فلا تجب عند اجتماع الشرائط. ومن السمعيات قوله تعالى : ولا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ [الأنعام: ١٠٣] والجواب بعد تسليم كون الإبصار للاستغراق وإفادته عموم السلب لا سلب العموم وكون الإدراك هو الرؤية مطلقا لا الرؤية على وجه الإحاطة بجوانب المرئى أنه لا دلالة فيه على عموم الأوقات والأحوال.
Thus He is seen not in a place, nor in a direction, nor by facing (muqabala), nor by connection of a ray (shu'aa'), nor by the existence of a distance between the seer and Allah the Most High; and analogizing the unseen (gha'ib) to the witnessed (shahid) is invalid (fasid). And one may argue for the lack of conditionality by Allah's seeing of us, but there is consideration in that, because the discussion is about vision by the sense of sight. And if it is said: if vision were possible and the sense are sound and all other conditions exist, it would necessarily be seen; otherwise it would be permissible that there are towering mountains in our presence that we do not see, and that would be sophistry. We say: it is not conceded, for vision with us is by Allah's creation (khalq), so it is not necessitated by the meeting of conditions. And among the scriptural objections is His statement, the Most High: 'Vision comprehends Him not, and He comprehends all vision' (Quran 6:103). The answer, after conceding that the word 'absar' is for generality (istighraq) and that it expresses universal negation (salb kulli) not negation of universality, and that comprehension (idrak) is vision absolutely (mutlaqan), not vision in the sense of encompassing all sides of the object, is that there is no indication in it for generality of times and states.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of both rational and scriptural objections against the vision of Allah (ru'ya). The author first states the correct doctrine: Allah is seen (in the Hereafter) not within a place, not in a direction, not by facing, not by connection of a ray of light, and not by a distance between the seer and Allah. The rationalist objection that vision requires these conditions is invalid because it is based on analogizing the unseen (gha'ib) to the witnessed (shahid), which is a false analogy. Then he addresses a rational argument: some argue that if ru'ya were possible, then when all conditions (sound sense, etc.) are present, vision would be necessary; otherwise it would be possible for mountains to exist before us unseen, which is absurd. The author replies that we do not concede that necessity: vision in our doctrine is by Allah's direct creation (khalq), so it does not become obligatory simply by the presence of conditions. Finally, he addresses the scriptural objection from Quran 6:103, 'Vision comprehends Him not.' The author answers by making two concessions: first, that the word 'absar' (vision) is for generality (istighraq), giving universal negation (no vision comprehends Him) rather than negation of universality (it does not mean that He is never seen). Second, that 'idrak' (comprehension) here means vision in an absolute sense, not vision that encompasses all aspects of the object. Even with these concessions, the verse does not indicate that vision is impossible at all times and in all states (i.e., it could be possible in the Hereafter). Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
مكان (makan)
جهة (jiha)
مقابلة (muqabala)
اتصال شعاع (ittisal shu'aa')
قياس الغائب على الشاهد (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid)
خلق الله (khalq Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If vision of Allah is possible and all conditions are met, it must necessarily occur; otherwise it would be absurd.
Correction: Vision is by Allah's direct creation, not necessitated by the presence of conditions. Allah creates vision at His will.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the rational objection that if vision were possible and conditions were present, it would be necessary?
A) He admits the objection and says ru'ya is impossible.
B) He says the conditions themselves are impossible for Allah.
C) He says vision is by Allah's creation and not necessitated by conditions.
D) He denies that vision requires any conditions.

Key Takeaway
Vision of Allah does not require physical conditions; it is directly created by Allah in the believer in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah will be seen without any physical conditions in the Hereafter, how does that belief affect your yearning for that vision? Are you actively seeking closeness to Allah now, or do you treat it as a distant theory?
?
The author says that rational analogies from this world fail when applied to Allah. In what specific area of your life are you still judging Allah's actions by worldly standards, expecting Him to act like a created being? What would it take for you to surrender that expectation?
?
When you face trials and cannot see Allah's wisdom, do you assume He is 'hidden' like physical objects behind a veil? How does this lesson's correction—that vision is by Allah's direct creation, not by conditions—challenge your impatience?

Answering the Scriptural Objection: 'Vision Comprehends Him Not'
Author answers the scriptural objection from Quran 6:103.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni theologians answer the scriptural objection from Quran 6:103 against the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
The verse denies comprehensive perception, not all vision; it does not negate ru'ya in the Hereafter.

Having addressed rational objections, the author now turns to the scriptural objection. The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (6:103) is often cited to deny any vision of Allah. The author provides a detailed answer by making two important concessions and then showing that the verse does not prove impossibility at all times. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural objection and answer
Read the portion of the passage that addresses the verse. Note the concessions the author makes.
2
Understand the two concessions
First, 'absar' is for generality (istighraq), giving universal negation. Second, 'idrak' means absolute vision, not comprehensive vision.
These concessions actually limit the meaning of the verse.
3
See the conclusion
Even with these concessions, the verse does not indicate that vision is impossible at all times and states. Thus it does not contradict ru'ya in the Hereafter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيرى لا فى مكان ولا في جهة من مقابلة أو اتصال شعاع ولا بثبوت مسافة بين الرائى وبين الله تعالى وقياس الغائب على الشاهد فاسد (٢). وقد يستدل على عدم الاشتراط برؤية الله تعالى إيانا وفيه نظر لأن الكلام فى الرؤية بحاسة البصر. فإن قيل لو كان جائز الرؤية والحاسة سليمة وسائر الشرائط موجودة لوجب أن يرى وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شاهقة لا نراها وأنها سفسطة .. قلنا ممنوع فإن الرؤية عندنا بخلق الله تعالى فلا تجب عند اجتماع الشرائط. ومن السمعيات قوله تعالى : ولا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ [الأنعام: ١٠٣] والجواب بعد تسليم كون الإبصار للاستغراق وإفادته عموم السلب لا سلب العموم وكون الإدراك هو الرؤية مطلقا لا الرؤية على وجه الإحاطة بجوانب المرئى أنه لا دلالة فيه على عموم الأوقات والأحوال.
Thus He is seen not in a place, nor in a direction, nor by facing (muqabala), nor by connection of a ray (shu'aa'), nor by the existence of a distance between the seer and Allah the Most High; and analogizing the unseen (gha'ib) to the witnessed (shahid) is invalid (fasid). And one may argue for the lack of conditionality by Allah's seeing of us, but there is consideration in that, because the discussion is about vision by the sense of sight. And if it is said: if vision were possible and the sense are sound and all other conditions exist, it would necessarily be seen; otherwise it would be permissible that there are towering mountains in our presence that we do not see, and that would be sophistry. We say: it is not conceded, for vision with us is by Allah's creation (khalq), so it is not necessitated by the meeting of conditions. And among the scriptural objections is His statement, the Most High: 'Vision comprehends Him not, and He comprehends all vision' (Quran 6:103). The answer, after conceding that the word 'absar' is for generality (istighraq) and that it expresses universal negation (salb kulli) not negation of universality, and that comprehension (idrak) is vision absolutely (mutlaqan), not vision in the sense of encompassing all sides of the object, is that there is no indication in it for generality of times and states.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of both rational and scriptural objections against the vision of Allah (ru'ya). The author first states the correct doctrine: Allah is seen (in the Hereafter) not within a place, not in a direction, not by facing, not by connection of a ray of light, and not by a distance between the seer and Allah. The rationalist objection that vision requires these conditions is invalid because it is based on analogizing the unseen (gha'ib) to the witnessed (shahid), which is a false analogy. Then he addresses a rational argument: some argue that if ru'ya were possible, then when all conditions (sound sense, etc.) are present, vision would be necessary; otherwise it would be possible for mountains to exist before us unseen, which is absurd. The author replies that we do not concede that necessity: vision in our doctrine is by Allah's direct creation (khalq), so it does not become obligatory simply by the presence of conditions. Finally, he addresses the scriptural objection from Quran 6:103, 'Vision comprehends Him not.' The author answers by making two concessions: first, that the word 'absar' (vision) is for generality (istighraq), giving universal negation (no vision comprehends Him) rather than negation of universality (it does not mean that He is never seen). Second, that 'idrak' (comprehension) here means vision in an absolute sense, not vision that encompasses all aspects of the object. Even with these concessions, the verse does not indicate that vision is impossible at all times and in all states (i.e., it could be possible in the Hereafter). Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
إدراك (idrak)
إبصار (ibsar)
سلب كلي (salb kulli)
عموم الأوقات والأحوال (umum al-awqat wa al-ahwal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' absolutely denies any possibility of seeing Allah in any form.
Correction: The verse denies comprehensive perception, not all vision; it does not negate vision in the Hereafter as the verse may be specific to this world or to a particular kind of vision.

Checkpoint
What does the author concede about the word 'absar' in the verse?
A) It is for generality, giving universal negation.
B) It is for negation of universality.
C) It means one specific vision.
D) It means comprehensive vision.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' does not prove impossibility of vision of Allah at all times; it only negates comprehensive perception or may be limited to this world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a verse of the Quran and felt a barrier between you and Allah, thinking that He is too distant or incomprehensible? How does this lesson's careful distinction between 'comprehension' and 'vision' invite you to reconsider that feeling?
?
If you knew for certain that you will see Allah in the Hereafter, how would that hope reshape your daily struggles and worship? What is holding you back from cultivating that certainty?
?
The author concedes the objector's interpretation but shows it still doesn't prove the conclusion. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that you've never examined deeply? Will you take the time to question them like this?

Argument for the Possibility of Seeing Allah
Author argues for the permissibility of seeing Allah using verse interpretation and rational reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife using scriptural and rational arguments.

Core Idea
The verse 'la tudrikuhu al-absar' implies permissibility, not impossibility.

This lesson explores a key theological argument for the Sunni doctrine that believers will see Allah in the Hereafter. The author carefully interprets a well-known Quranic verse and addresses objections, showing that the possibility of vision is not only permissible but also indicated by early Muslim reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author uses both rational and scriptural reasoning.
2
Understand the verse interpretation
The author argues that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' actually supports permissibility because praising its negation would be meaningless if seeing were impossible.
Key distinction between seeing (ru'ya) and perception (idrak).
3
Examine the rebuttal to objections
The author addresses the objection that asking to see Allah is met with disapproval, explaining it is due to obstinacy, not impossibility.
Reference to Moses and the idol request.
4
Reflect on evidence from disagreements and dreams
The disagreement among Companions about the Prophet seeing Allah during Mi'raj and reports of dream visions further indicate possibility.
Disagreement about occurrence implies possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل بالآية على جواز الرؤية إذ لو امتنعت لما حصل التمدح بنفيها كالمعدوم لا يمدح بعدم رؤيته لامتناعها وإنما التمدح في أنه تمكن رؤيته، ولا يرى للتمنع والتعزز بحجاب الكبرياء وإن جعلنا الإدراك عبارة عن الرؤية على وجه الإحاطة بالجوانب والحدود، فدلالة الآية على جواز الرؤية، بل تحققها أظهر ولان المعنى أن الله تعالى مع كونه مرئيا لا يدرك بالأبصار، لتعاليه عن التناهي والاتصاف بالحدود والجوانب . ومنها : أن الآيات الواردة في سؤال الرؤية مقرونة بالاستعظام والاستنكار. والجواب : أن ذلك لتعنتهم وعنادهم في طلبها، لا لامتناعها. وإلا لمنعهم موسى عليه السلام عن ذلك، كما فعل حين سألوا أن يجعل لهم آلهة (١) . فقال : إنكم قوم تجهلون ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٣٨] وهذا مشعر بإمكان الرؤية في الدنيا ولهذا اختلف الصحابة رضى الله عنهم في أن النبي هل رأى ربه ليلة المعراج أم لا ؟ والاختلاف في الوقوع دليل الإمكان. وأما الرؤية فى المنام فقد حكيت عن كثير من السلف . ولا خفاء في أنها نوع مشاهدة تكون بالقلب دون العين
And it is sometimes argued by the verse for the permissibility of seeing [Allah], for if it were impossible, then praise for negating it would not occur, just as the non-existent is not praised for not being seen because of its impossibility; rather, praise is for that which can be seen, but is not seen due to being prevented and glorified by the veil of majesty. And if we make perception (idrāk) an expression for seeing in the manner of encompassing sides and limits, then the indication of the verse for the permissibility of seeing, rather its occurrence, is more apparent. And because the meaning is that Allah, exalted, despite being visible, is not perceived by sight, due to His transcendence beyond finitude and being characterized by limits and sides. And among them [the objections]: that the verses about asking to see are accompanied by awe and disapproval. The answer: that is due to their obstinacy and stubbornness in requesting it, not because of its impossibility. Otherwise, Moses (peace be upon him) would have prevented them from that, as he did when they asked to have a god made for them, saying: 'Indeed, you are a people acting ignorantly.' [7:138] And this indicates the possibility of seeing in this world. For this reason, the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) differed on whether the Prophet saw his Lord on the night of the Mi'raj or not. And disagreement about occurrence is evidence of possibility. As for seeing in a dream, it has been reported from many of the Salaf. And there is no doubt that it is a kind of vision that occurs by the heart, not the eye.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents a rational and scriptural argument for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife. The author begins by interpreting the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision perceives Him not) as actually implying permissibility: if it were impossible, there could be no praise in negating it, like praising something that cannot be seen. The praise indicates the thing can be seen but is not seen due to divine majesty. The author then distinguishes between seeing (ru'ya) and perception (idrāk), explaining that Allah is visible but not encompassed by sight due to His transcendence. Next, he addresses the objection that the Quranic verses where people ask to see Allah are met with disapproval. He argues that the disapproval is due to their obstinacy, not the impossibility of the request, citing Moses' response when his people asked for an idol—he rebuked them for ignorance but did not say the request was impossible. This indicates the possibility of seeing in this world. The author then notes that the Companions disagreed on whether the Prophet saw Allah during the Mi'raj, and such disagreement about actual occurrence proves possibility. Finally, he mentions that many Salaf reported seeing Allah in dreams, which is a vision of the heart, not the eye. The overall argument supports the Sunni doctrine that believers will see Allah in the hereafter.

إدراك
جواز
تمدن
إمكان

Misconception: Some think the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' negates all possibility of seeing Allah.
Correction: The author shows it only negates comprehensive perception (idrak), not vision itself, and actually implies permissibility.

Checkpoint

B) Because praising the negation of seeing would be meaningless if seeing were impossible.
C) Because the verse is abrogated.
D) Because the Companions agreed on it.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing Allah is supported by textual and rational arguments, and the verse negates comprehensive perception, not vision itself.

Scriptural Proof: Allah Creates All Actions
Scriptural proof using Quranic verses that God creates actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses, especially 37:96, 13:16, and 16:17, provide scriptural evidence that Allah creates human actions, and why this proof does not depend on a specific grammatical interpretation.

Core Idea
The Quran explicitly states that Allah creates all things, including our deeds.

After establishing rational proofs, the author now turns to scriptural evidence. The Quran contains clear verses that affirm Allah's creation of all things, including human actions. This lesson examines the key verses and addresses a common grammatical subtlety that might confuse the reader.

Guided Path
1
Read the main verse: 37:96
Read the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' and note the two possible interpretations of 'ma'.
The word 'ma' can be a masdariyya (making it 'your deed') or a relative pronoun (making it 'your actions').
2
Understand the author's clarification on 'act'
The author distinguishes between the infinitive meaning of 'act' (bringing into existence) and the product of the act (the observed movements). The verse refers to the product.
This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.
3
Examine the supporting verses
Look at verses 13:16 and 16:17. They show that Allah is the Creator of all things, and that creating is a basis for worship.
These verses reinforce that human actions are included in 'all things'.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm that the proof does not depend on one grammatical interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني (۱) : النصوص الواردة فى ذلك . كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصافات: ٩٦] أى عملكم على أن ( ما ) مصدرية لئلا يحتاج إلى حذف الضمير، أو معمولكم على أن «ما» موصولة . ويشتمل الأفعال لأنا إذا قلنا : أفعال العباد مخلوقة الله تعالى أو للعبد ؟ لم نرد بالفعل المعنى المصدرى، الذى هو الإيجاد والإيقاع، بل الحاصل بالمصدر الذى متعلق الإيجاد والإيقاع، أعنى ما نشاهده من الحركات والسكنات مثلا والذهول عن هذه النكتة قد يتوهم أن الاستدلال بالآية موقوف على كون ( ما ) مصدرية. كقوله تعالى: ﴿ اللَّهُ خَالِقُ كُلِّ شيء [الرعد: ١٦] أي ممكن، بدلالة العقل وفـعـل الـعـبـد شي ممكن وكقوله تعالى : أَفَمَن يَخْلُقُ كَمَن لا يخلق ﴾ [النحل : ۱۷] في مقام دح بالخالقية وكونها مناطا لاستحقاق العبادة .
The second (proof): The textual evidences concerning that. Such as His statement, Exalted: 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Sūrat al-Ṣāffāt, 96) — meaning 'your deed' if 'mā' is considered a masdariyya, so as not to need the deletion of the pronoun; or 'your actions' if 'mā' is a relative pronoun. And it includes actions, for when we say: The actions of the servants — are they created by Allah, Exalted, or belong to the servant? We do not intend by 'action' the infinitive meaning, which is bringing into existence and effecting, but rather the product of the infinitive that is the object of bringing into existence and effecting, namely what we observe of movements and rests, for example. And heedlessness of this subtlety may lead one to imagine that seeking evidence with the verse is dependent on 'mā' being masdariyya. Like His statement, Exalted: 'Allah is the Creator of all things' (Sūrat al-Ra‘d, 16) — meaning all possible things, by indication of the intellect, and the action of the servant is a possible thing. And like His statement, Exalted: 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' (Sūrat al-Naḥl, 17) — in the context of proving the attribute of creating and its being the basis for deserving worship.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This is the second proof, which is scriptural (naqlī). It cites the Quranic verse: 'And Allah created you and what you do' (37:96). The author explains that the word 'mā' can be interpreted either as a masdariyya (making the phrase 'your act') or as a relative pronoun (meaning 'your actions'). Either way, it includes the actions of humans, indicating they are created by Allah. The proof does not depend on the grammatical interpretation of 'mā' as masdariyya; additional verses like 'Allah is the Creator of all things' (13:16) and 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' (16:17) support that all possible things, including human actions, are created by Allah.

ما مصدرية
ما موصولة
فعل

خلق كل شيء

Misconception: Some think the proof from 37:96 depends on 'ma' being interpreted as masdariyya (making it 'your deed').
Correction: The author shows that even if 'ma' is a relative pronoun (meaning 'your actions'), the verse still indicates creation of actions. Additionally, other verses like 13:16 and 16:17 independently support the same conclusion.

Checkpoint

A) Because the verse can be understood either way and still indicates creation of actions.
B) Because the Mu'tazila deny the verse altogether.
C) Because the masdariyya interpretation is invalid.
D) Because the verse only applies to non-human actions.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verses 37:96, 13:16, and 16:17 collectively prove that Allah creates all things, including human actions, regardless of grammatical nuances.

Nature of Death: Existent or Non-Existent?
Debate on whether death is existent or non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether death is an existent entity or merely a non-existent decree, and the evidence used by the majority.

Core Idea
Death is not a created thing but a decree of non-existence.

This passage tackles a metaphysical question: Is death a real created entity or simply the absence of life? The author presents two views, one supported by a Quranic verse, and then clarifies the majority interpretation that death is non-existent and 'created' means 'decreed'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on death’s nature
Read the Arabic passage that discusses whether death is existent or non-existent. Notice the Quranic evidence and the majority opinion.
Focus on the first part of the source text.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the view that death is existent (supported by the verse) and the majority view that it is non-existent. Understand the reasoning behind each.
3
Understand the majority interpretation
The majority interprets 'created death' as 'He decreed death'. Reflect on why they consider death non-existent rather than a created entity.
This aligns with the Ash'ari position that non-existence is not a thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومبنى هذا على أن الموت وجودى، بدليل قوله تعالى : خلق الموت والحياة [الملك: ٢] والأكثرون على أنه عدمى ومعنى خلق الموت : قدره . والأجل واحد لا كما زعم الكعبي ، أن للمقتول أجلين : القتل والموت . وأنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى أجله الذي هو الموت. ولا كما زعمت ( الفلاسفة ) أن للحيوان أجلا طبيعيا. وهو وقت موته بتحلل رطوبته وانطفاء حرارته الغريزيتين وآجالا اخترامية - على خلاف مقتضى طبيعته - بحسب الآفات والأمراض.
And the basis of this is that death is existent, as evidenced by His saying, the Most High: 'He created death and life' [Quran 67:2]. And the majority hold that it is non-existent, and the meaning of 'created death' is He decreed it. And the term is one, not as al-Ka'bi claimed: that the killed has two terms: killing and death, and that if he were not killed he would live to his term which is death. And not as the philosophers claimed: that animals have a natural term, which is the time of their death by the dissolution of their moisture and extinguishing of their innate heat, and interceptive terms, contrary to the requirement of their nature, according to afflictions and diseases.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage discusses the metaphysical nature of death and the unity of the term. It begins by stating the basis of the previous discussion (about the killed dying by his term) is whether death is existent (wujudi) or non-existent ('adami). The author notes that some argue death is existent, citing the Quranic verse 'He created death and life' (Surah al-Mulk, 67:2) as evidence that death is created, hence existent. However, the majority (al-aktharun) hold that death is non-existent, and they interpret 'created death' to mean 'decreed it' (qaddarahu), not that it is a created entity.

The author then affirms that the term (ajal) for everyone is one and the same, refuting two alternative views. First, al-Ka'bi (a Mu'tazilite) claimed that the killed person has two terms: the term of killing (i.e., the time he is killed) and the term of death (i.e., the time he would have died naturally). According to him, if he were not killed, he would live until the second term which is death. The author rejects this, insisting the term is one.

Second, the philosophers (al-falasifa) are refuted. They claim that animals have a natural term (ajal tabi'i), which is the time of death caused by the dissolution of their two moistures and extinguishing of their two innate heats (i.e., natural aging). Additionally, they posit 'interceptive terms' (ajal ikhtirami) that occur contrary to the natural requirement, due to accidents and diseases. The author rejects both multiplicity of terms and the philosophical distinction between natural and accidental terms.

Key Terms
الموت
الوجودي
العدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think death is a created entity like life, based on the literal reading of 'created death'.
Correction: The majority holds that death is non-existent; 'created' here means 'decreed', not brought into existence as an entity.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view in this passage, what does 'He created death' mean?
A) God brought death into existence as a created entity.
B) God decreed death, as it is non-existent.
C) God created the concept of death in the mind.
D) God created the time of death as a separate entity.

Key Takeaway
The majority of Islamic theologians consider death to be non-existent and interpret 'created death' as 'decreed death'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If death is merely the cessation of life and not a created entity, does your fear of death shift from the unknown state to the moment of passing itself? How does that affect your readiness for it?
?
The majority interpret 'He created death' as 'He decreed death'. Do you live with the conscious certainty that your death is decreed? What would change in your daily actions if you truly internalized that?
?
If death is not a thing that exists, what does that imply about the afterlife? Does this deepen your hope in meeting Allah, or does it confront you with the reality of non-existence? How do you respond to that challenge?
